Set:nlt.",\nNATIONAL BUREAU OF STANDARDS.\n\nErneat~.\n\nDIIwctw\n\nPROCESSING/MICROSTRUCTURE/PROPlRTY\nRELATIONSHIPS IN 2024 ALUMlNUM\nALLOY PLATES\nABSTRACT\nNondestructive evaluation (NDE) using eddy-current conductivity\nand hardness measurements form an essential part of the quality co~tro1\nof aluminum alloy plates used for aerospace vehicles. The relationships\nbetween the NDE measurements and the important mechanical properties\nare affected by a large number of variables including: chemical composition, cast structure, ingot scalping, solution heal treatment a~d\nquenching, mechanical working, and aging treatment. At the request of\nthe National Aeronautics and Space Administration, a number of these\nrelationships has been explored for 2024 aluminum alloy. This work is\na continuation of our previous efforts on 2219 aluminum alloy and is\nmotivated by a concern that a number of improperly treated plates with\nIIsoft spots II may have been incorporated into aerospace structures. A\nmajor result or our research has been a delineation of which alloy\ntempers and plate thicknesses are most likely to contain IIsoft spotsll\ndue to specific p\'ocessing errors.\nThe\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ninvesti~dtion\n\nincluded the following:\n\nStudies on as-received material. This included a 15.24 cm \\S in.)\nthic~ plate of 2024-T851, an 0.635 cm (~/4 in.) thick plate of\n2024-F, and a direct chill cast ingot for 2024, all obtained from\nindustry.\nInvestigation of the phases and inclusions present in cast 2024\naluminum alloy with the aim of determining the degree of microand macro-segregation and identifying the inclusions present in\nthe as-cast ingots.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDetermination of a set of C-curves which can predict the mechanical\nand NDE properties for any type of quench following the solution\nheat treatment. Two tempers, T851 and T351, were investigated.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTransmission electron microscope studies of the stable and metastable phases present and an attempt to re1at~ the observed microstructural changes to the measured changes in mechanical properties\nand NDE measurements.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nA study of the ultrasonic wave propagation as a fur~tion of thermomechanical treatment of the alloy with the objet~ive of establishing\n~ correlation between ultrasonic data and mechanical properties\nand of providing an additional NDE method to improve the characterization of the material.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nA nondestructive evaluation of the age hardening sequence by means\nof dynamic eddy-current conductivity measurements.\n\nii\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nUse of a heal flow model lo calculate almost all conceivable heat\nflow conditions anlicipated during the qllench of 2024 aluminum\nalloy plate from the solution heat treatment temperature of 495\xc2\xb0C.\nThe calculated time-temperature data were then coupled to the\nC-curves and the variations in properties acrOS3 different thickness\nplates for\' the "worst case" heat flow conditions were predicted.\n\nPredictable macrosegregation was obtained in laboratory ingots\nof 2024 aluwinum alloy. It was found that macros~gregation of copper\nand other alloying additions in direct chill cast ingots 0f 2024\naluminum alloy cannot be completely eliminated by t~ill face scalping\nand subsequent thermomechanical treatment. Although good scalping\npractice should maintain compositions within specified limits with no\ndeteriorat;on in mechanical properties, the macrosegregation remaining\nin the finished plate product will contribute to the scatter observed\nin NDE measurements. Further, because of the large copper content\nvariation near the chill face, surface hardness and eddy-current\nconductivity measurements are necessarily very sensitive to scalping\ndepth in their ability to evaluate the condition of finished alloy\nplat.es.\nA large number of samples were taken from a 0.635 cm thick\nplate of 2024 aluminum alloy in the F temper and processed to the\nT851, T351 or T4 temper. During processing to these tempers, the\nquench following solution heat treatment was varied, giving a series\nof samples with a wide range of microstructures and hence mechanical\nproperties. Two types of lipre-aging" treatment, labeled sequence A\nand sequence B. wet~e used. The hardness, eddy-current cond Ictivity,\nyield strength, ultimate tensile strength, elongation, and area\nreduction of these samples were measured. The accumulated data were\nusad to establish a set of approxiruate C-curves from which the alloy\nproperties can be established for any time-temperature cycle of the\nquench following heat tr~atment. The C-curvej can also be used to\ngenerate correlation3 between mechanical and NDE properties. It was\nfound t.hat ed~y-current conductivity alone cannot be used as a reliable\npredictor of the mechanical properties of 2024-T351 or 2024-T851. It\nmust be combined with other information such as hardness dnd yield\nstrength measurements on the same lot (same in! t or plate) of material.\nThe C-curves were combined with time-temperature data from a\ncomputerized heat flow model to predict the variations in properties\nacross plates of different thicknesses for both sequence A and\nsequence B type IIpre-agingll heat treatments. It was found that the\nT851 temper is quite "quench sensitiv\xe2\x82\xac\'" in the sense that the\nultimate tensile strength falls below specification for the "worst\n~ase" quench conditions for rather thin plates (~ 1 cm thick).\nTEM studies were carried out on a large number of specimens\nin an 120 kV in~trument equipped to 0perate in th~ scanning\ntransmission (STEM) mode as well as in the cunventional t~ansmission\nmode. The instrument was equipped with an x-ray energy dispersive\nspectrometer. A major objective was to identify those microstructural\nchanges that were responsible for the mechdnical and physical propel\'ties\n\niii\n\ndelineated in the C-curve representations. Although the microstructure\nas a function of "pre-aging" t)\'eatment is complex, it appears that\n"pre-aging" induced precipitates can contribute to the strength and are\nsubject to overaging during further heat treatment. This provides a\nmechan~sl\' for the loss of streng~h which can account for the fp~t that\nthe str \xe2\x80\xa2. igth of the T851 temper is actually reduced below that of the\n1351 temper given the same "pre-aging" heat treatment (rather than\nbeing raised as occurs for properly qu~nched material).\nThe oLjective of the ultrasonic studies was to determine the extent\nto which a correlatfon exists between the mech~nical and ultrasonic\nproperties. For this purpose, the absolute, rather than the relative,\nvalues of the sound velocity and ultrasonic attenuation ar~ req~ired.\nThe absolute values of sound velocity and ultrasonic attenuation were\ndetermined to within \xc2\xb1 1 ms 1 and \xc2\xb1 0.02 dB, respectively. A parabolic\nrelationship was found between hardness and sound-wave velocity, whereas\nultrasonic attenuation decreases with increasing hardness.\n\n.a\n\niv\n\nlABLE OF CONTENTS\n\n. . . . . . . .\n\n1.\n\n~NTRODUCTION.\n\n:1.\n\nSTUDIES ON AS-RECEIVED PLATES OF 2024\n\n1\nALU~INUM\n\nALLOY.\n\nI I 1. AJuminum\n(a)\n\nCalculation of\n\nMicroseg~~gation\n\nA~\n\nfor Al-Cu-Mg-Mn-Fe-S; Alloy\n\nSystem\n~alculatlon\n\nof expected microsegregation for the n-component alloy\n\nwas performed using the solidi-\n\nfication of primary a-aluminum. the situation is governed by (n-l)\ndifferential equations (2)\ndfL\ndeli\n\n=\n\nfl\n\n1\n----\n\n= I.\n\n... n-l\n\n[ 1]\n\nl-k~ C\n1\nLi\n\nwhere fL is conductivity, yield strength,\n\nultimate tensile strength and time-temperature history have been used\nto determine a set of C-curves for both sequence A and sequence B\n\n, I\nI\n\nalloys and for tempers T851 and T351.\nThe C-curves are a family of C-shaped curves used to characterize\nthe effects of quench rate on the final properties of the finished\nmaterial.\n\nTheir use for aluminum alloys was pioneered by W L. Fink\n24\n\n:1\n\nand L. A. Wi lley\n\nThese curves represent the effect of IIpl\'e-agi ngll\n\n(7).\n\ntime at a given temperature\n\n(int~rmediate\n\nbetween the solution heat\n\ntreatment temperature and the quench water temperature) on the final\nproperties of the material.\n\nIn using these curves to determine the\n\neffect of an ay\'bitrary time-temperature history duri ng the quench from\nsolution heat treatment temperature, a "rule of additivity" developed\nby Cahn (8) is used.\n\nBasically, this rule states that the degradation\n\nin. for example. yield strength that occurs in a given temperature\ninterval is independent of that which occurred during previous temperature intervals passed through during the quench.\n\nThe total degradation\n\nis taken as the simple sum of the degradations occurring in each temperature interval during the quench.\n\nThis rule is also used in determination\n\nof the C-curve but is much less important there because the "pre-aging"\nis made as nearly isothermal as possible.\nThe extent to which the "rule of additivity" is applicable to\naluminum alloys has not bp.en established.\n\nHowever, previous experience\n\nby Staley (9) indicates that it can be applied with good approximation\nat least to some aluminum alloys.\n\nOne might expect the rule to apply\n\napproximately when the degradation in properties is small and when the\ntemperature decreases monotonically.\n\nThe differences we find in the\n\npresent study between the sequence A and sequence B heat treatments\ngives some indication as to how closely this rule is followed.\nthe previous and T851 Conditions\n1.\n\nIntroduction\nThe study 01 ultrasonic wave propagation Cin provide informatior,\n\nabout the elastic properties\n\n~nd\n\nabsorption characteristics of the\n\nmaterial in which the wave propagates.\n\nThese properties arp, intimately\n\nrelated to microstructure, hence the thermomechanical treatment that\nthe material underwent prior to the ultrasonic measurements, thus\nenabling a nondestructive evaluation and characterization of the materials\npropert ies.\nTh. speed of sound ina so 11 d is most often used in nondes truct,i ve\ntesting as a constant programmed into an ultrasonic thickness gauge.\nHowever, in the realm of nondestructive characterization of\n\nr"aterial~,\n\nthe absolute measurement of longitudinal and shear wave velocities can\nbe used to calculate many useful\n\nmat~rial\n\nparameters, e.g., Young\'s\n\nmodulus (E), shear modu1us (G), bulk modulus (8), and Poisson\'s ratio\n(u).\n\nThe determination of these effective moduli is based on the\n\nassumptior. that the material is isotropic.\n\nThe calculation of the\n\nmoduli requires knowledQe of the value for the density of the material.\nThe energy loss, or ultrasonic attenuation, of elastic waves propagating in a solid, may be divided into contributions from geometrical and\nintrinsic effects.\n\nGeometrical effects include reflection and refraction\n\nat a free surface, grain or phase boundary, beam divergence due to diffraction, as well as waveguide effects dup. to multiple boundary surfaces.\nIntrinsic effects\n\nin~lude\n\nscattering of the\n\nultra~Jnic\n\nwave at inhomogene-\n\nities, interaction with thermal phonons, dislocation damping, and conversion of sound energy to heat as a result of elastic deformation.\n\n68\n\n:1\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nUnlike geometric effects. intrinsic effects\n\nm~\n\nchange in time if\n\nthe internal cryst.al lattice or defect structure undergoes change.\nOurin~\n\nprecip11.ation hardening of 2024 aluminum alloy the main contribu\xc2\xb7\n\ntion to the attenuation .s believed to be scattering of the ultrasonic\nwave by newly precipitating phases.\n\nThe magnitude of the absorption\n\nwill be determined by the magnitude of the stress field at the boundary\nbetween the precipitate and the matrix which. in turn. is determined by\nthe precipitate size and the degree of coherency of the precipitate\nwith the matrix (41).\n\nAn attenuation peak should be observed when the\n\nsize of the precipitate and associated stress field around it reach a\nmagnitude that produces the maximal interaction with the ultrasonic\nwave.\n\nThis value of att@.nuation will also depend on the wavelength of\n\nthe ultrasonic wave relative to the size of the scattering center.\n\nAs\n\nfrequency ircreases. the wavelength decrt!ases. and hence the size of an\neffective scattering center will a, ) decrease.\nWith continuous monitoring. the relationship between\nand change in\n\n~ound\n\nagin~\n\ntime\n\nvelocity and ultrasonic attenuation can be determined.\n\nThe information may provide an insight into the kinetics and mechanism\nof precipitate formation a;ld growth in the age-hardening aluminum\nalloys.\n\nSeveral investigations (41-43) have demonstrated that the\n\nultrasonic method is operationally feasible f0r monitoring the precipitation process in aluminum alloys in real time, over a wide temperature\ninterval.\n\nThe calculated activation energies and characteristic kinetic\n\nparameters could be related to the mechanism of the formation and\ngrowth of the precipitates, and their\npropert 1 es of the alloy.\n\n~ffect\n\non the ultimate mechanical\n\nThe extens i on of techni ques for ultras"ni c\n\nNDE to industrial applications requiring on-line, real-time monitoring\n\n69\n\nduring thermoMechanical treatMent wOI,ld necessitate Modificatfons of\nthe experfmental approach for the s"lIcific technological applfcation.\nThe objective of the present study was to establish the existence\nof a correlation between ultrasonic data and mechanical properties\n(e.g. hardness, yield strength) of 2024 aluminum alloy specimens after\nthey had been subjected to carefully controlled thermomechanical treatments.\n\nFor this purpose, the absolute, rather than the relatiJe,\n\nvalues of the .ound velocity and ultrasonic attenuation are required.\nThe data have properties of the\n\nDld~r\n\nalloy.\nAs will be shown, a correlation between the absolute values of the\nsound velocity and ultrasonic attenuation, and the hardness data was\nfound.\n\nRegardless of the thermal sequence\n\n~f\n\nthe aging process, maximal\n\nhardness (and strength) of the 2024 aluminum alloy was found to be\nrelated to a definite range in the values of the sound velocity.\n\nA\n\nconsistent differen:e between the sequence A and B IIpre-agingll heat\nti~eatments\n\nwas reVl a 1ed.\n\nFurtherml>re, a di fference was a 150 noted in\n\nthe values of the sound intentionally left blank\n\nVIII.\n\nHEAT FLOW-PROPERTY PREDICTIONS\n\nA heat flow model developed earlier for 2219 aluminum alloy (1)\nwas used here to calculate almost all conceivable heat flow conditions\nanticipated during the quench of 2024 aluminum a110y plates from the\nsolutionizing temperature of 495\xc2\xb0C.\n\nThe calculated time-temperature\n\ndata was then coupled to the C-curves established in section IV.\nvariations in properties across\n\ndiffer(~nt\n\nThe\n\nthickness plates for the\n\nworst and the best heat flow conditions were thus predicted.\n1.\n\n:ieat Flow Model\nThe heat flow calculations carr1ad out for the various "pre-aging"\n\ntreatments during cooling of a flat plate from an initial temperature\nof To were identical to\n\nthos~\n\npreviously described (1).\n\nThey included\n\nthe fa 11 owi ng:\n(a)\n\nAsymmetri: cooling of plates, where heat is withdrawn from\n\noniy th\xe2\x82\xac top surface for all times.\n(b)\nby\n\nSymmetric cooling of plates from both top and bottom followed\n\nan abrupt variation in the heat transfer coefficient on\n\nth~\n\nbottom\n\nsurface of the plate, at different times.\nTime-temper"ature data from the computer program was then combined\nwith equations (3) to (5) for the determination of C-curves using the\nvalues of the constants reported in Table V.\n\nThe numerical procedure\n\nfor the determination of a given property, e.g., yield strength, was as\nfollows.\n\nEquation (4) is integrated, using the calculated time-\n\ntemperature data and equation (5) for a given\nand the quantity K1XtX is determined.\n\npositio~\n\nin the plate,\n\nUsing the values of am and 00\n\nfrom Table V, the value of a, in this case yield strength, is\n\nestab1ish~d.\n\nThese computations are carried out numerically and simultaneously with\nthe heat flow calculations.\n90\n\nFinally, for a given sequence (A or B) and a given heat treatment,\nthe values of the constants K , K4 and K5 are identical for all pro~erties\n3\nin question, while om\' 00 and K2 differ according to Table V.
